Taking More Than Mahr When the Wife Is Nashizah
Ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and dual dalil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi rivayat on taking more than the mahr when the wife is nashizah and explain the dalil for each view.

Core Idea
When the wife is nashizah, taking more than the mahr is makruh in al-Mabsut but permissible in al-Jami' al-Saghir.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi legal difference within the works of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. The core scenario is khula' initiated due to the wife's nushuz. While taking exactly the mahr is agreed upon as permissible without karahah, the ruling on taking excess differs between two of Imam Muhammad's own compilations: al-Mabsut and al-Jami' al-Saghir. Each view relies on a different primary evidence, and understanding the reconciliation is key to grasping Hanafi methodology.

Guided Path
1
State the agreed-upon ruling
When the wife is nashizah (disobedient) and the husband seeks khula', taking exactly the mahr (or its equivalent) is permissible without any karahah. Identify this as the base ruling.
This is not disputed.
2
Identify the point of ikhtilaf
The dispute is about taking more than the mahr. Al-Mabsut holds it is makruh; al-Jami' al-Saghir holds it is ja'iz (permissible).
Both are Zahir al-Riwayah works, so the ikhtilaf is within the school's core corpus.
3
Analyze the dalil for the Mabsut view
The evidence for makruh is the hadith of Thabit ibn Qays's wife, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'As for the extra (ziyada), no.' This indicates prohibition or at least disapproval.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted as evidence.
4
Analyze the dalil for the Jami' al-Saghir view
The evidence for permissibility is the Quranic verse: 'fala junaha 'alayhima fima iftadat bihi' (they are not blameworthy for what she ransoms herself with). This verse is absolute (mutlaq) and includes little and much without restriction.
The absoluteness of the verse supports taking any amount.
5
Note the resolution (mufta bihi)
The relied-upon view, as suggested by the passage's phrasing and the order of presentation, appears to be the Mabsut ruling (makruh). However, both views are valid within the school.
The author uses 'karehna' (we consider makruh) which indicates the predominant teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر عورت ناشزہ ہے تو مرد کیلئے دیئے ہوئے سے زیادہ وصول کرنا مکروہ ہے
وان كان النشوز منها كرهنا له ان ياخذمنها اكثر مما اعطاها وفى رواية الجامع الصغير طاب الفضل ايضا لاطلاق ماتلونا بدأو وجه الاخرى قوله عليه السلام في امرأة ثابت بن قيس بن شماس اما الزيادة فلا وقد كان
النشوز منها
ترجمہ اور اگر سرکشی عورت کی جانب سے ہو تو ہم شوہر کے لئے مکر وہ جانتے ہیں کہ بیوی سے اس سے زیادہ لے جو اس کو دیا ہے اور جامع صغیر کی روایت میں ہے کہ زائد لینا بھی جائز ہے اس آیت کے مطلق ہونے کی وجہ سے جو سابق میں حلاوت کر چکے اور دوسری ، روایت کی وجہ ثابت بن قیس بن شماس کی بیوی کے بارے میں حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا قول اما الزیادۃ فلا ہے حالانکہ سرکشی بیوی ہی کی طرف سے تھی ۔
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ عورت کی جانب سے اگر شرارت ہو تو مقدار مہر تک لینا شوہر کے لئے بلا کراہت جائز ہے مگر مقدار مہر سے زائد لینا مبسوط کی روایت کے مطابق مکروہ ہے اور جامع صغیر کی روایت کے مطابق جائز بلا کراہت ہے۔ عدم کراہت کی دلیل وہ آیت ہے جو سابق میں گزر چکی ہے یعنی فلا جناح عليهما فيما افتدت به اور یہ آیت اپنے اطلاق کی وجہ سے قلیل اور کثیر مبر
If the disobedience is from the wife, then we consider it makruh for the husband to take from her more than what he gave. And in the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, taking excess is also permissible due to the absoluteness of the verse we recited before, and the other aspect is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, regarding the wife of Thabit ibn Qays ibn Shammas: 'As for the excess, no.' And indeed the disobedience was from her.

Translation: And if the disobedience is from the wife's side, then we consider it makruh for the husband to take from her more than what he gave. And in the narration of Jami' al-Saghir, taking excess is also permissible due to the absoluteness of the verse that has passed before, and the other narration is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, regarding the wife of Thabit ibn Qays ibn Shammas: 'As for the excess, no.' While the disobedience was from her side.

Explanation: The scenario is that if from the wife's side there is mischief, then up to the amount of mahr it is permissible for the husband to take without karahah. But taking more than the mahr, according to the narration of al-Mabsut, is makruh, and according to the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is permissible without karahah. The proof of no karahah is the verse that has passed before, i.e., 'fala junaha 'alayhima fima iftadat bihi', and this verse, due to its absoluteness, includes little and much...

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf when the wife is nashizah. The main ruling: taking exactly the mahr is permissible without karahah. Taking more than the mahr is viewed differently in two rivayat: according to al-Mabsut, it is makruh; according to al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is permissible without karahah. The evidence for permissibility is the general verse 'fala junaha 'alayhima fima iftadat bihi'. The evidence for karahah is the Hadith of Thabit ibn Qays where the Prophet forbade taking extra. The commentator explains that the verse is absolute, while the Hadith restricts it.

Key Terms
مہر (mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife at marriage; in khula', the wife may give back her mahr or more.
روایت (riwayah): A transmission or narration within the Hanafi school; here referring to different opinions in different books of Imam Muhammad.
مطلق (mutlaq): Unrestricted, general; used here for the Quranic verse that is absolute in allowing any amount of compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration of al-Mabsut, what is the ruling on taking more than the mahr when the wife is nashizah?
A) Haram and invalid
B) Makruh tanzih
C) Ja'iz without karahah
D) Wajib

Key Takeaway
When the wife is nashizah, taking more than the mahr is makruh in the relied-upon Hanafi position, though a valid rivayah permits it.

Ruling on Husband's Nushuz and Taking Compensation
States the ruling and proofs when husband is nashiz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the husband taking compensation when he is the nashiz, explain the proof from Quran 4:20, and apply the distinction between nahy li dhatihi and nahy li ghayrihi.

Core Idea
When the husband is nashiz, taking compensation from the wife is makruh but valid—the prohibition is external.

This lesson examines a critical nuance in Hanafi family law: when the husband is the one showing discord (nushuz), can he accept compensation from the wife for divorcing her? Based on Quran 4:20, the Hanafi school derives that it is makruh (disliked) but not haram, due to the prohibition being external (nahy li ghayrihi). Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between a prohibition that invalidates an act and one that merely discourages it.

Guided Path
1
Define nushuz from the husband
Identify that nushuz (discord) can originate from either spouse. Here, the husband is the nashiz—the one showing displeasure or transgression.
2
State the ruling for the husband
When the husband is nashiz, it is makruh for him to take any compensation from the wife in exchange for divorce. However, if he does take it, the divorce is valid.
3
Analyze the primary proof (Quran 4:20)
The verse says 'do not take anything' when replacing a wife. The Hanafi school interprets this prohibition as nahy li ghayrihi (external prohibition)—it does not affect the intrinsic validity of the contract.
Contrast with nahy li dhatihi which would make the act haram and invalid.
4
Apply the analogy with Friday trade
The text compares to buying and selling during the Friday adhan: the act is valid in itself but prohibited due to an external reason. Similarly, taking compensation is valid but disliked due to the husband's transgression.
5
Note the second rational proof
The husband should not increase the wife's distress by taking her wealth after already causing her harm by bringing another wife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الخلع تشریح نشوز عورت کا اپنے شوہر کی نافرمانی کرنا اور اس سے نفرت کرنا۔ زجاج فرماتے ہیں کہ نشوز دونوں طرف سے ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی زوجین میں سے ہر ایک کا دوسرے سے ناگواری ظاہر کرنا۔ صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر کی جانب سے نشوز اور ناگواری کا اظہار ہو تو شوہر کے لئے بدل ضلع کے طور پر عورت سے کچھ لینا مکروہ ہے۔ دلیل باری تعالیٰ کا قول ہے : و ان اردتم استبدال زوج مکان زوج و أتيتم احدهن قنطارا فلانا خذوامنه شياً. انا خذونه بهتانا واثما مبينا اور اگر تم نے ایک بیوی کی جگہ دوسری بیوی بدلنے کا ارادہ کیا ، حالانکہ تم نے ایک کو ڈھیر بھر دے رکھا ہے تو تم اس میں سے کچھ مت لو ۔ کیا تم اس کو لیتے ہو تہمت لگا کر اور صریح گناہ کے مرتکب ہو کر ۔ قنطار بیل کی کھال بھر سونا یا چاندی اور بعض نے کہا کہ ستر ہزار دینار اور بعض کہتے ہیں کہ ایک ہزار دو سواد قیہ اور ایک اوقیہ چالیس درہم کا ہوتا ہے اور زمخشری نے کہا کہ قطار مال عظیم کو کہتے ہیں بہر حال اس آیت میں عورت سے عوض لینے کی کراہت پر صراحت موجود ہے۔ اس لئے عورت سے عوض لینا مکروہ قرار دیا گیا۔ البتہ اگر شوہر نے لے لیا تو کراہت کے باوجود جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ فلا تاخذ وامنہ شیا میں نہی لغیرہ ہے اور نہی لغیرہ کی وجہ سے مشروعیت ذاتی معدوم نہیں ہوتی ۔ جیسے جمعہ کے دن اذان کے وقت خرید وفروخت کرنا مشروع لذاتہ اور ممنوع الغیرہ ہے۔ دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس شخص نے سابقہ بیوٹی کی جگہ دوسری بیوی لا کر اس کو وحشت میں ڈال دیا ہے۔ اس لئے اب اس سے مال سابقہ لا اسکو لے کر مزید وحشت میں نہ ڈالا جائے۔
Chapter of Khul'. Explanation: Nushuz of a woman is disobeying her husband and hating him. Zajaj says: Nushuz occurs from both sides, i.e., each spouse expresses displeasure to the other. The ruling: If nushuz and displeasure is from the husband's side, then it is makruh for the husband to take anything from the wife as compensation. Evidence is the saying of Allah Most High: 'And if you desire to replace a wife with another and you have given one of them a great treasure, do not take anything from it. Would you take it with slander and manifest sin?' (4:20). Qintar is a skinful of gold or silver, some say seventy thousand dinars, some say one thousand two hundred uqiyas and one uqiya is forty dirhams, and Zamakhshari said qintar means abundant wealth. In any case, this verse clearly states the dislike (karahah) of taking compensation from the wife. Therefore, taking compensation from the wife is deemed makruh. However, if the husband takes it, it is permissible despite the karahah, because 'do not take anything from it' is a prohibition due to an external reason (nahy li ghayrihi), and an external prohibition does not negate the intrinsic validity of the act. Like buying and selling at the time of the Friday adhan is legitimate in itself but prohibited due to an external reason. The second proof is that this person has brought a second wife instead of the first, causing her distress; therefore, he should not increase her distress by taking her wealth.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines nushuz and then presents the ruling when the husband is the nashiz (the one showing discord). The ruling is that it is makruh (disliked) for the husband to take any compensation from the wife in exchange for divorcing her. The primary proof is Quran 4:20, which is interpreted as a prohibition due to an external reason (nahy li ghayrihi), meaning the act of taking compensation is not inherently invalid but is disliked because of the context of the husband's transgression. The passage explains that even if the husband takes it, it is valid (ja'iz) but still makruh, analogous to conducting trade during the Friday sermon. A second rational proof is given: the husband should not add to the wife's distress by taking her wealth.

Key Terms
نشوز (nushuz): Rebellion or discord from either spouse; here specifically the husband's transgression.
نہی لغیرہ (nahy li ghayrihi): A prohibition due to an external factor, not affecting the intrinsic permissibility of the act itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the husband is the one showing nushuz, what is the ruling on him taking compensation from the wife?
A) It is haram and invalid.
B) It is makruh but valid if done.
C) It is permissible without dislike.
D) It is recommended.

Key Takeaway
When the husband is nashiz, taking compensation is makruh but valid; the prohibition does not affect the contract's validity because it is external.

Ruling on Wife's Nushuz: Taking More than Mahr
Intra-Hanafi dispute on taking excess mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on taking excess mahr when the wife is nashizah and evaluate their evidence.

Core Idea
Two conflicting riwayat within the Hanafi school: al-Mabsut rules it makruh, al-Jami' al-Saghir permits it.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: when the wife is rebellious (nashizah) and seeks khul', may the husband take more than the mahr he gave? The source presents two conflicting riwayat from the Zahir al-Riwayah corpus—al-Mabsut ruling it makruh and al-Jami' al-Saghir ruling it permissible. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens our ability to navigate different textual transmissions within the school and apply the mufta bihi view in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the passage deals with a wife's nushuz (rebellion) and the husband's right to take property in exchange for releasing her through khul'.
The husband may take up to the mahr without dispute; the disagreement is over taking more.
2
State the two positions
From al-Mabsut: taking excess is makruh. From al-Jami' al-Saghir: taking excess is permissible without dislike.
Both are from Zahir al-Riwayah, but they differ.
3
Analyze the evidence for permissibility
The verse 'There is no sin upon them for what she ransoms herself with' (Quran 2:229) is absolute (mutlaq) and includes all amounts, thus supporting permissibility.
This is the dalil for the view of al-Jami' al-Saghir.
4
Analyze the evidence for dislike
The Prophet's statement regarding the wife of Thabit ibn Qays, 'As for the excess, no,' indicates negation of excess, implying dislike. This is the dalil for al-Mabsut's view.
The hadith is detailed earlier in the chapter.
5
Determine the thamarah and mufta bihi
The practical difference is whether the husband commits a disliked act. The text leans towards al-Mabsut's view, making it the relied-upon position.
Later Hanafi authorities prefer the makruh view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان كان النشوز منها كرهنا له ان ياخذمنها اكثر مما اعطاها وفى رواية الجامع الصغير طاب الفضل ايضا لاطلاق ماتلونا بدأو وجه الاخرى قوله عليه السلام في امرأة ثابت بن قيس بن شماس اما الزيادة فلا وقد كان النشوز منها ترجمہ اور اگر سرکشی عورت کی جانب سے ہو تو ہم شوہر کے لئے مکر وہ جانتے ہیں کہ بیوی سے اس سے زیادہ لے جو اس کو دیا ہے اور جامع صغیر کی روایت میں ہے کہ زائد لینا بھی جائز ہے اس آیت کے مطلق ہونے کی وجہ سے جو سابق میں حلاوت کر چکے اور دوسری ، روایت کی وجہ ثابت بن قیس بن شماس کی بیوی کے بارے میں حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا قول اما الزیادۃ فلا ہے حالانکہ سرکشی بیوی ہی کی طرف سے تھی ۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ عورت کی جانب سے اگر شرارت ہو تو مقدار مہر تک لینا شوہر کے لئے بلا کراہت جائز ہے مگر مقدار مہر سے زائد لینا مبسوط کی روایت کے مطابق مکروہ ہے اور جامع صغیر کی روایت کے مطابق جائز بلا کراہت ہے۔ عدم کراہت کی دلیل وہ آیت ہے جو سابق میں گزر چکی ہے یعنی فلا جناح عليهما فيما افتدت به اور یہ آیت اپنے اطلاق کی وجہ سے قلیل اور کثیر مبر اور غیر مہر سب کو شامل ہے ۔ اور مقدار مہر پر زیادتی کے مکروہ ہونے کی دلیل ثابت بن قیس بن شماس کی بیوی سے حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا ارشاد واما الزيادة فلا ہے۔ یعنی حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے زیادتی کی نفی فرمادی ہے اور جب اباحت منکی ہو گئی تو کراہت ثابت ہو جائے گی یہ حدیث بالتفصیل اول باب میں آچکی ہے۔
And if the rebellion is from her, we consider it makruh for him to take from her more than what he gave. And in the riwaya of al-Jami' al-Saghir, the surplus is also permissible due to the absoluteness of the verse we recited, and another aspect is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding the wife of Thabit ibn Qays: 'As for the excess, no,' even though the rebellion was from her. Translation: And if the rebellion is from the wife, we consider it makruh for the husband to take from his wife more than what he gave her. And in the riwaya of al-Jami' al-Saghir, taking the excess is also permissible due to the absoluteness of the verse mentioned earlier. The other evidence is the Prophet's statement about the wife of Thabit ibn Qays: 'As for the excess, no,' even though the rebellion was from her. Explanation: The ruling is that if the wife is the one causing trouble, taking up to the amount of mahr is permissible without dislike, but taking more than the mahr is makruh according to the riwaya of al-Mabsut, and permissible without dislike according to the riwaya of al-Jami' al-Saghir. The proof for the absence of dislike is the verse mentioned earlier, i.e., 'There is no sin upon them for what she ransoms herself with' (2:229), and this verse, due to its absoluteness, includes little and much, and other than mahr. And the proof for the dislike of taking excess is the Prophet's statement regarding the wife of Thabit ibn Qays: 'As for the excess, no.' That is, the Prophet negated the excess, and when permissibility is removed, dislike is established. This hadith has been detailed earlier in the chapter.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where the wife is the nashizah (rebellious). It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: According to the riwaya of al-Mabsut (one of the core books), it is makruh for the husband to take more than the mahr he gave. According to the riwaya of al-Jami' al-Saghir (another core book), taking extra is permissible without any dislike. The text explains the evidence for each view: For permissibility, the absolute wording of Quran 2:229 ('There is no sin upon them for what she ransoms herself with') is cited. For the dislike, the hadith of Thabit ibn Qays's wife is presented, where the Prophet said 'As for the excess, no,' implying prohibition of taking more than the original mahr. The commentary notes that this hadith is mentioned in detail earlier. The passage thus highlights a disagreement within the Hanafi school on this point.

Key Terms
مبسوط (al-Mabsut): One of the six core books of Zahir al-Riwayah by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
جامع صغیر (al-Jami' al-Saghir): One of the six core books of Zahir al-Riwayah by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
نشوز (nushuz): Rebellion or disobedience of the wife towards her husband's legitimate rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Mabsut, what is the ruling on the husband taking more than the mahr when the wife is nashizah?
A) It is permissible without dislike.
B) It is makruh.
C) It is haram.
D) It is recommended.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two positions on taking excess mahr from a nashizah wife; the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that it is makruh.

The Distinction Between Jawaz and Ibahah: Judicial Validity Despite Karahah
Explains the coexistence of jawaz and karahah in taking excess mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that taking excess mahr is ja'iz (valid) yet makruh, using the distinction between jawaz and ibahah.

Core Idea
Legal validity (jawaz) and moral permissibility (ibahah) are distinct; an act can be valid in court yet religiously disliked.

When a husband takes more than the mahr in a khul' settlement, is the transaction valid or invalid? The Hanafi school answers with a nuanced distinction: it is legally valid (ja'iz) but religiously disliked (makruh). Understanding this requires grasping the difference between jawaz and ibahah. In this lesson, you will see how the Hanafi jurists reconcile the Qur'anic permission with a conflicting hadith, producing a ruling that upholds judicial validity while acknowledging religious disapproval.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific act being ruled upon? What hukm level does the passage assign (ja'iz but makruh)?
The act is taking more than the mahr in a khul' or similar context.
2
Locate the dalil
Which proof is cited? Here, Quran 2:229 and a hadith of Thabit are mentioned.
The Quranic verse is the primary proof; the hadith serves as a conflicting evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the verse imply both jawaz and ibahah? How does the hadith restrict only the ibahah aspect?
The verse generally permits taking excess, implying both legal validity and moral permissibility. The hadith specifically negates the excess, removing ibahah but leaving jawaz.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and resolution
The conflict between the verse and hadith regarding ibahah is resolved by prioritizing the hadith for ibahah, but the verse remains operative for jawaz, as no contrary evidence exists for that aspect.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of applying a proof as far as possible, not abandoning it entirely when a conflict arises.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
What is the mufta bihi? The husband may take extra, but it is makruh. Why is this the relied-upon position?
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, and there is no intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر مرد نے مہر سے زیادہ وصول کر لیا تو قضاء لینا جائز ہے ولو اخذ الزيادة جاز فى القضاء و كذالك اذا اخذ والنشوزمه لان مقتضى ماتلون شيئان الجواز حكما والاباحة وقدترك العمل فى حق الاباحة لمعارض فبقی معمولا في الباقي ترجمہ اور اگر ( مہر سے زائد لے لیا تو قضاء جائز ہے اور اسی طرح جب کہ لیا حالانکہ سرکشی شوہر کی جانب سے ہے کیونکہ ہم نے جو آیت تلاوت کی اس کا متنی دو چیریں ہیں ایک تو حکماً جائز ہونا اور دوسرے مباح ہونا اور اباحت کے حق میں معارض کی وجہ سے عمل متروک ہو گیا تو باقی کے حق میں ( آیت پر ) عمل رہا۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ عورت کے ناشزہ ہونے کی صورت میں اگر شوہر نے عورت سے مقدار مہر سے زیادہ لے لیا تو قضاء جائز ہے اور ایسے ہی اگر سرکشی شو ہر کی طرف سے ہے اس کے باوجود شوہر نے مقدار مہر سے زیادہ لے لیا تو بھی قضاء جائز ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ فلا جناح عليهما فيما افتدت به - آیت کا مقتضی دو چیزیں ہیں (۱) شرعاً جواز (۲) اباحت یعنی حلت اور اباحت اور جواز میں فرق یہ ہے کہ اباحت کی ضد کراہت ہے اور جواز کی ضد حرمت ہے۔ مگر جو مباح ہوگا وہ جائز ضرور ہو گا لیکن جائز کے لئے مباح ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے۔ مثلاً بمعہ کے دن اذان کے وقت خرید و فروخت کرنا جائز ہے۔ مگر مباح نہیں بلکہ مکروہ ہے معلوم ہوا کہ جواز اور کراہت دونوں جمع ہو سکتے ہیں۔ اب طریق استدلال یہ ہوگا کہ ! احت کے حق میں معارض یعنی حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کے قول اما الزيادة فلا کی وجہ سے آیت معمول بہانہ رہی اور چونکہ جواز کے حق میں کوئی معارض موجود نہیں اس لئے جواز کے حق میں آیت پر عمل باقی رہے گا کیونکہ اباحت کی نفی جواز کی نفی کو مستلزم نہیں ہے۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ مقدار مہر سے زائد لینا شوہر کے لئے جائز تو ہے مگر مباح نہیں بلکہ کراہت ہے۔
If the husband took more than the mahr, then judicially it is valid to take it. And likewise if he took it while the rebellion is from the husband, because the implication of the verse we recited are two things: permissibility by law (jawaz hukman) and permissibility (ibahah). And the operation (of the verse) regarding ibahah was abandoned due to a contrary evidence, so it remains operative in the other aspect. Translation: And if (he took more than the mahr), then judicially it is valid, and likewise when he took it while the rebellion is from the husband, because the verse we recited implies two things: jawaz hukman and ibahah. And the practice regarding ibahah was abandoned due to a conflicting proof, so the verse remains operative in the remaining aspect. Explanation: The ruling is that if the wife is nashizah and the husband takes more than the mahr, it is judicially valid. Similarly, if the husband is the nashiz and he takes more, it is also judicially valid. The proof is that Quran 2:229 implies two things: jawaz (legal validity) and ibahah (permissibility). The difference between ibahah and jawaz is that the opposite of ibahah is karahah, while the opposite of jawaz is hurmah. Whatever is mubah is necessarily ja'iz, but ja'iz does not have to be mubah. For example, buying and selling at the time of the Friday adhan is ja'iz but not mubah; rather it is makruh. Thus, jawaz and karahah can coexist. The reasoning: regarding ibahah, there is a conflicting evidence (the Prophet's statement 'as for the excess, no'), so the verse is not acted upon for ibahah, but since there is no conflict for jawaz, it remains operative for jawaz. Therefore, it is established that taking more than the mahr is ja'iz for the husband, but not mubah; rather it is makruh.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage clarifies that even if taking excess than the mahr is considered makruh, it is still judicially valid (ja'iz fi al-qada). The key analytical distinction is between jawaz (legal validity) and ibahah (moral permissibility). The opposite of jawaz is hurmah (prohibition), while the opposite of ibahah is karahah (dislike). An act can be ja'iz (legally valid) yet makruh (disliked), like trading during Friday sermon. The verse (2:229) implies both, but the conflicting hadith of Thabit overrides only the ibahah aspect, leaving jawaz intact. Therefore, if a husband takes extra, the transaction is valid in court (qada) even if it is religiously disliked.

Key Terms
جواز (jawaz): Legal validity; the act is recognized by law and does not require repetition.
اباحت (ibahah): Moral permissibility; the act is free from religious dislike.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct relationship between jawaz and ibahah?
A) They are synonymous.
B) Jawaz is opposite of karahah; ibahah is opposite of hurmah.
C) Jawaz is opposite of hurmah; ibahah is opposite of karahah.
D) Both are opposites of hurmah.

Key Takeaway
An act can be legally valid (ja'iz) yet religiously disliked (makruh); the Hanafi school uses this distinction to uphold court validity while acknowledging spiritual disapproval.

Ruling on Khul' Divorce: Effect and Obligation of Payment
States the ruling on khul' divorce and its effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of khul' divorce according to Hanafi fiqh and explain why the payment becomes obligatory upon acceptance.

Core Idea
Khul' is an irrevocable divorce that makes the wife's payment obligatory upon acceptance.

This passage from Al-Hidayah lays down the foundational ruling of khul' divorce: the moment the wife accepts the conditional offer, both the divorce and the financial obligation take immediate effect. The author justifies the ruling by highlighting the husband's capacity for conditional divorce, the wife's legal competence to undertake payment, and the nature of khul' as an exchange of wealth for the marriage bond. Understanding this ruling is essential for distinguishing khul' from other forms of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is khul': the husband divorces his wife in exchange for a payment, and the wife accepts. The ruling: the divorce occurs immediately, and the payment becomes obligatory (wajib) on the wife.
2
Identify the basis for the divorce taking effect
The husband has the power to pronounce divorce both as an immediate (munajaz) and as a conditional (mu'allaq) divorce. Here, he made the divorce conditional upon her acceptance. When she accepts, the condition is fulfilled and the divorce takes effect.
Conditional divorce is valid in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Identify the basis for the wife's obligation to pay
The wife has legal capacity (wilaya 'ala nafsiha) to undertake financial obligations. Moreover, the marriage contract is something that can be terminated for compensation, similar to qisas (where blood money can be accepted instead of retaliation).
The analogy to qisas reinforces that even non-property rights can be exchanged.
4
Recognize the type of divorce resulting
The divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) because it is an exchange of wealth (the payment) for the self (the marriage bond). Since the husband gives up his right to the marriage, the wife gives up her payment, achieving equality. They cannot remarry without a new marriage contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان طلقها على مال فقبلت وقع الطلاق ولزمها المال لان الزوج يستند بالطلاق تنجيزا وتعليقا وقد علقه بقبولها والمرأة تملك التزام المال لولايتها على نفسها وملك النكاح مما يجوز الاعتياض عنه وان لم يكن مالا كالقصاص وكان الطلاق بائنا لما بينا ولأنه معاوضة المال بالنفس وقدملك الزوج احد البدلين فتملك هى الأخر وهو النفس تحقيقا للمساواة ترجمہ اور اگر اس کو شوہر نے مال پر طلاق دی پھر اس نے قبول کر لی تو طلاق واقع ہوگئی اور عورت پر مال لازم ہو گیا ۔ کیونکہ شوہر
And if he divorces her for a payment and she accepts, the divorce occurs and the payment becomes obligatory on her, because the husband is capable of divorce with immediate effect and as a conditional matter, and he made it conditional upon her acceptance. The woman has the authority to obligate herself with payment because she has guardianship over herself, and the ownership of marriage is something that can be exchanged for compensation even if it is not property like qisas. And the divorce is irrevocable (ba'in) as we have explained, and because it is an exchange of wealth for the self, and the husband has given one of the two exchanges, so she possesses the other, which is her self, to achieve equality.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage states the basic ruling of khul': when the husband pronounces divorce in exchange for a payment and the wife accepts, the divorce takes effect immediately and the wife owes the payment. The reasoning: the husband has the power to issue divorce both immediately and conditionally; here he conditions it on her acceptance. The wife has legal capacity to undertake a financial obligation because she is capable of managing her own affairs. Marriage is a contract that can be terminated by compensation, similar to qisas (where blood money is exchanged). The divorce becomes a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce because it is an exchange of wealth for the wife's right (the marriage bond). Since the husband gives up his right (the marriage), the wife gives up her payment, achieving parity.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): Irrevocable divorce after which the husband cannot take back the wife without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In khul', if the husband says 'I divorce you for a thousand dirhams' and the wife accepts, what type of divorce occurs?
A) Raj'i (revocable) divorce
B) Ba'in (irrevocable) divorce
C) Both A and B
D) The divorce does not occur

Key Takeaway
Khul' divorce is a valid exchange that results in an irrevocable divorce and makes the wife's payment obligatory upon acceptance.

Divorce upon Payment: Binding Effect and Bain Nature
Ruling on conditional divorce upon acceptance of compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband divorces his wife on payment of a sum and she accepts, and explain why the divorce is bain (irrevocable) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Khul' is an exchange: the husband gets the payment, the wife gets her freedom — hence the divorce is irrevocable.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya explains the Hanafi ruling on a conditional divorce tied to a payment by the wife. It establishes that when the wife accepts the condition, both the divorce and the payment become binding. The author also provides the rationale for why such a divorce is bain (irrevocable), emphasizing the transactional nature of the arrangement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Pinpoint the precise legal scenario: the husband pronounces divorce conditioned on the wife's payment, and she accepts. The ruling: the divorce occurs and the payment becomes binding.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The proof cited includes the husband's independent authority to give conditional divorce, the wife's capacity to undertake payment, and the nature of marriage as a compensable right (like qisas).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
The husband suspends talaq on acceptance; acceptance fulfills the condition. The transaction is a mu'awadah (exchange). The marriage tie is compensable, as seen in qisas.
4
Note the nature of divorce (bain vs raj'i)
Because the wife gives property in exchange for her freedom, the divorce must be bain (irrevocable) to achieve equality — the husband cannot retain partial rights.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position: the divorce is bain and the payment is binding; the husband cannot revoke during the iddah without a new marriage contract.

And if he divorces her upon payment and she accepts, the divorce occurs and the payment becomes binding upon her. Because the husband is independent in giving divorce unconditionally or conditionally, and he has made it conditional upon her acceptance. And the wife has the capacity to undertake payment because she has authority over herself. And the ownership of marriage is something that can be compensated, even if not property, like qisas. And the divorce is bain (irrevocable) for what we have explained, and because it is an exchange of property for the self, and the husband has become owner of one of the two substitutes, so she becomes owner of the other, which is the self, to achieve equality. Translation: And if the husband divorces her upon payment and she accepts, divorce occurs and the payment becomes binding on her. Because the husband is independent in giving divorce unconditionally or conditionally, and he has made it conditional upon her acceptance. And the wife has the authority to undertake payment because she has authority over herself. And the ownership of marriage is a thing whose compensation is permissible, even if it is not property, like qisas. And this divorce will be bain (irrevocable) due to the evidence we have already stated, and because it is an exchange of the self for property, and the husband has become owner of one of the two substitutes, so the wife becomes owner of the other, which is the self, so that equality is established. Elucidation: The scenario is that if the husband divorces his wife upon payment, e.g., he says 'You are divorced for a thousand dirhams' or 'You are divorced upon a thousand dirhams', then the wife accepts, the divorce occurs and the payment becomes binding on her. The evidence is that this is a transactional disposition, and a transactional disposition is contingent on the capacity of the two parties and the suitability of the subject matter, and all these are present. The husband's act is established because he has independent authority to give divorce unconditionally or conditionally, and here he has made divorce conditional on her acceptance, so her acceptance is a condition. If she accepts, divorce occurs and payment becomes binding; otherwise not. The wife's capacity is established because she has full authority over her own self to undertake payment. And the subject matter is suitable because the ownership of marriage is a thing whose compensation is permissible, even if not property, like qisas, although it is not property, but its compensation is permissible. And the common link between them is the undertaking of the capable person. And when divorce occurs for payment, it will be bain. One evidence has already passed: the wife will accept payment only when her self is handed over, and that is achieved by bain divorce. For this reason, bain divorce occurs. The second evidence is that this divorce for payment is an exchange of property for the self: the wife intends to give property to her husband in return for freeing herself, and the husband becomes owner of one substitute, i.e., the property, so the wife becomes owner of the other substitute, i.e., the self, to achieve equality. And a woman can become owner of her self only through bain divorce, not through raj'i divorce, because in raj'i divorce the husband's right is not severed. So it is established that divorce for payment results in bain divorce.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of divorce (talaq) conditioned upon payment by the wife. The author states that if a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced for such amount' or 'upon such amount', and the wife accepts, then talaq occurs and the payment becomes binding on her. The ruling is based on the nature of this transaction as a mu'awadah (exchange). The husband has independent authority to talaq; here he suspends the talaq on the wife's acceptance. The wife has full authority (wilayah) over herself, so she can validly undertake the payment. The subject matter (mahall), i.e., the marital bond (milk al-nikah), is capable of being exchanged for compensation, even if the compensation is not property (mal) but rather something like qisas (retaliation). This shows that the marriage tie is considered a property-like right in the Hanafi school. The divorce that occurs is bain (irrevocable) because it is a compensation for the release of the wife's self (nafs): the husband gets the property, the wife gets her freedom. In raj'i talaq, the husband retains the right of return, which contradicts the idea of full exchange. Therefore, the divorce must be bain. The commentator emphasizes that both conditions are required: the husband's capacity to talaq, the wife's capacity to undertake payment, and the suitability of the marital bond as compensable. The example of qisas shows that a non-property right can be taken in exchange, as in qisas where the victim's heirs can accept blood money.

Key Terms
طلاق بائن (ṭalāq bā'in): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back the wife without a new marriage contract.
معاوضہ (mu'āwaḍah): A transaction involving an exchange of two counter-values (badalayn).
بدل (badal): The counter-value given in exchange; in khul', one badal is the wife's payment, the other is the husband's relinquishment of marital rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the divorce in khul' considered bain (irrevocable) according to this passage?
A) Because the wife's payment automatically makes the husband's right to return invalid.
B) Because it is an exchange (mu'awadah) where the husband receives the property and the wife receives her self, which cannot be returned partially.
C) Because the Prophet declared all khul' divorces as bain.
D) Because the wife's acceptance is a condition that makes the divorce bain by default.

Key Takeaway
A divorce upon payment (khul') is binding and results in a bain (irrevocable) divorce because it is an exchange of property for the self.

Invalid Compensation in Khul': Ruling and Distinctions
Ruling when the compensation (badal) in khul' is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khul' with invalid consideration and explain the difference from talaq with invalid consideration.

Core Idea
If the compensation in khul' is invalid, the separation is still valid (bain) but the husband gets nothing.

In a khul' divorce, the wife offers compensation to the husband in exchange for her release. But what if that compensation is something Islamically impermissible, like wine or a pig? This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies that the khul' itself remains effective, but the husband forfeits the compensation, and the separation is irrevocable (bain). The ruling hinges on whether the expression used is explicit (sarih) or allusive (kinayah), and on the nature of the wife's undertaking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the case involves a Muslim husband making khul' with his wife in exchange for wine, pig, or carrion—items that are not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) in Islamic law.
These items are not considered property for Muslims, hence invalid as compensation.
2
Determine the legal effect on the divorce
Note that the khul' itself is valid and results in a bain (irrevocable) divorce. The husband receives no compensation. This is the core ruling.
In contrast, if compensation is invalid in talaq, the divorce becomes raj'i (revocable).
3
Analyze the reasoning
Understand why the husband gets nothing: The wife did not name valuable property that would make her liable for deception (ghurur). There is no way to enforce the named compensation (due to its invalidity) nor to impose something else (no undertaking).
The operation of the word 'khul' ' (kinayah) means the divorce occurs without the compensation condition.
4
Distinguish from analogous cases
Contrast with: (a) khul' upon vinegar that turns out to be wine – here the wife is liable for the value due to ghurur; (b) kitabah or emancipation in exchange for wine – the master gets the value of the slave because his ownership is valuable; (c) marriage with invalid dower – different reasoning.
In khul', the husband's right to the wife's pudendum (bud') upon separation is not considered valuable property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وان بطل العوض فى الخلع مثل ان يخالع المسلم على خمر او خنزير او ميتة فلاشئ للزوج والفرقة بائنة و ان بطل العوض في الطلاق كان رجعيا فوقوع الطلاق فى الوجهين للتعليق بالقبول وافتراقهما في الحكم لانه لما بطل العوض كان العامل فى الأول لفظ الخلع وهو كناية وفى الثانى الصريح وهو يعقب الرجعة و انما لم يجب للزوج شيء عليها لانها ما سمت ما لا متقوما حتى تصير غارة له ولانه لاوجه الى ايجاب المسمى للاسلام ولا الى ايجاب غيره لعدم الالتزام بخلاف ما اذا خالع على خل بعينه فظهرانه خمر لانها سمت مالافصار مغرورا وبخلاف ما اذا كاتب او اعتق على خمر حيث تجب قيمة العبدلان ملك المولى فيه متقوم ومارضى بزواله مجانا اماملك البضع فى حالة الخروج غير متقوم على مانذكر وبخلاف النكاح
He said: And if the compensation in khul' is void, such as a Muslim making khul' upon wine, or pig, or carrion, then nothing is due to the husband, and the separation is bain. And if the compensation is void in divorce, then it becomes raj'i. So the occurrence of divorce in both cases is due to the suspension upon acceptance, but they differ in ruling. Because when the compensation is void, the operative word in the first (khul') is the word khul', which is a kinayah (allusive expression), and in the second (talaq) it is the explicit word, which entails the possibility of return (ruj'ah). And nothing is due to the husband from her because she did not mention valuable property such that she becomes a wrongdoer. And there is no way to impose the named compensation due to its being forbidden, nor to impose something else due to lack of undertaking. Unlike if he made khul' upon a specific vinegar and it turned out to be wine, because she mentioned property (mal) and thus deceived him. And unlike if he made kitabah (contract of manumission) or freed a slave upon wine, where the value of the slave is due, because the master's ownership in him is valuable, and he did not consent to its disposal for free. But the ownership of the pudendum (al-bud') at the time of exit is not valuable, as we mentioned. And unlike marriage.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal consequences when the consideration (badal) in khul' is invalid, specifically when the compensation is not a valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) according to Islamic law. Examples include wine, pig, and carrion, which are not considered property for Muslims. The ruling: the husband receives nothing, and the separation is bain (irrevocable). The commentator then distinguishes this from divorce (talaq) where if the compensation is void, the divorce becomes raj'i (revocable). The reason: in khul', the divorce is effected by the word 'khul', which is kinayah (allusive), and when the compensation is void, the divorce still occurs but without the condition of compensation, so the original intent (separation) is still achieved, but since the compensation fails, the separation is bain as per the nature of khul'. In talaq, the word 'talaq' is explicit and conveys power of return (ruj'ah) unless compensation is valid. The text then explains why nothing is due: because the wife did not name a valuable property that would make her liable for guroor (deception). There is no way to enforce the named compensation (since it's invalid) nor to impose a different compensation (since she did not undertake it). The text gives a contrast: if khul' was upon specific vinegar that turned out to be wine, she must pay the value because she named a valuable property and thus deceived him. Another contrast: if a master contracts kitabah (manumission on payment) or emancipates a slave in exchange for wine, the value of the slave must be paid because the master's ownership is valuable property and he did not consent to losing it for free. But in khul', the husband's ownership of the wife's pudendum (bud') upon exit is not considered valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim), so the rule differs. Finally, it contrasts with marriage (nikah) where similar reasoning applies (presumably in marriage contracts with invalid dower).

Key Terms
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwam): Valuable property according to Shari'ah; items like wine and pig are not mutaqawwim for Muslims.
کنایہ (kināyah): An allusive expression that implies divorce but requires intention or context; not explicitly 'talaq'.
غرور (ghurūr): Deception that leads to liability; here if the wife names something as property while it is not, she is liable via ghurur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Muslim husband makes khul' with his wife in exchange for a bottle of wine, what is the ruling according to this passage?
A) The khul' is void, and the marriage continues.
B) The khul' is valid, the husband must be given the wine, and the divorce is raj'i.
C) The khul' is valid, the separation is bain, and the husband gets nothing.
D) The khul' is valid, the husband is entitled to the value of the wine, and the divorce is bain.

Key Takeaway
When compensation in khul' is invalid, the divorce is effective and irrevocable, but the husband receives no compensation.

Talaq — Divorce

Khul': A Commutative Transaction Resulting in Ba'in Divorce
Ruling on divorce in exchange for property with conditions and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that a khul' divorce is ba'in and to identify the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
Khul' is a commutative transaction: the wife pays property to obtain a ba'in divorce.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on divorce in exchange for property, known as khul'. The text from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the legal reasoning behind the validity of such a divorce and its irrevocable nature. We will explore the conditions, the proof, and why the divorce is ba'in.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is khul': the husband says 'You are divorced for a specified property' and the wife accepts. The ruling is that the divorce is valid and ba'in, and the wife owes the property.
2
Analyze the conditions
The conditions for validity: the husband must have the authority to divorce, the wife must accept the offer, and the property must be specified.
Acceptance can be verbal or by action (e.g., paying the amount).
3
Examine the proof
The text gives two proofs: (a) khul' is a commutative transaction (tasarruf mu'awada), which requires capacity of parties and suitability of subject matter, both present here; (b) it is an exchange of property for the self, requiring equality of ownership, achieved only through ba'in divorce.
The first proof establishes validity; the second establishes the irrevocability.
4
Understand the result
The divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) because only then does the wife gain full ownership of herself, creating equality in the exchange.
In raj'i divorce, the husband retains rights, breaking the equality.
5
Note cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may consider khul' a different type of dissolution or allow it to be revocable, but the Hanafi position is based on the commutative nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الخلع
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد پنجم طلاق منجز یا طلاق معلق دینے میں مستقل ہے اور حال یہ ہے کہ اس نے طلاق کو عورت کے قبول کرنے پر معلق کیا ہے اور عورت کو اپنے ذمہ مال لازم کر لینے کا اختیار ہے۔ کیونکہ اس عورت کو اپنے نفس پر ولایت حاصل ہے اور ملک نکاح ایسی چیز ہے کہ اس کا عوض لینا جائز ہے اگر چہ وہ مال نہ ہو جیسے قصاص اور یہ طلاق بائنہ ہوگی اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم بیان کر چکے اور اس لئے کہ یہ ذات کا معاوضہ مال سے ہے اور حال یہ کہ شوہر احد البد لین کا مالک ہوا۔ پس عورت دوسرے کی مالک ہوگی اور وہ (اس کی ) ذات ہے تا کہ ( دونوں میں ) برابری ثابت ہو۔

Chapter of Khul'. Ashraf al-Hidaya, Urdu commentary of Hidaya, volume five. The husband is independent in giving an absolute or conditional divorce, and the situation is that he has made the divorce conditional upon the wife's acceptance, and the wife has the authority to make property obligatory upon herself. Because this woman has full authority over her own self, and the ownership of marriage is such that taking compensation for it is permissible even if it is not property, like qisas. And this divorce will be ba'in due to the proof that we have already mentioned, and because this is an exchange of the self for property, and the husband has become the owner of one of the two compensations. So the wife will become the owner of the other, and that is her self, so that equality is established. Explanation: The scenario is that if the husband divorces his wife for property, for example he says 'You are divorced for a thousand dirhams' or 'You are divorced upon a thousand dirhams', then the wife accepts it, the divorce will occur and the property will become obligatory upon the wife. The proof is that this is a commutative transaction, and a commutative transaction depends on the capacity of the two transactors and the suitability of the subject matter, and all these exist. The husband's action is proven because he has the absolute authority to give an immediate or conditional divorce at the moment, and here he has made the divorce conditional upon the wife's acceptance. Therefore her acceptance is a condition: if she accepts, the divorce will occur and the property will be obligatory upon her; otherwise not. The wife's capacity is proven because she has the right to make property obligatory upon herself, as she has full authority over her own self. The subject matter is suitable because the ownership of marriage is something for which taking compensation is permissible, even if it is not property, like qisas: although it is not property, taking compensation for it is permissible. And between the two there is a comprehensive link because both parties are capable of undertaking obligations. And when divorce occurs in exchange for property, it will be ba'in. One proof has already passed: that the wife will only accept property upon herself when her self is surrendered to her, and this is achieved by ba'in divorce. For this reason, the divorce will be ba'in. The second proof is that this divorce in exchange for property is an exchange of property for the self, meaning the wife intends to give property to her husband to free her self, and the husband has become the owner of one compensation (property), so the wife will become the owner of the other compensation (the self) to establish equality. And the wife can only become owner of her self through a ba'in divorce, not through a raj'i divorce, because in a raj'i divorce the husband's right is not severed. So it is established that divorce in exchange for property results in a ba'in divorce.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya is an expansion on the ruling of khul' (divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation). The author explains that when a husband says to his wife, 'You are divorced for a thousand dirhams' or similar, and she accepts, the divorce is valid and she must pay the amount. This is because it is a commutative transaction: the husband gives up his marital rights, and the wife gives property. Both parties have the capacity: the husband has the right to divorce, and the wife can obligate herself. The subject matter (marriage) is suitable for compensation, as even non-property rights like qisas can be compensated. The divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) because only through ba'in divorce does the wife gain full ownership of herself; in raj'i divorce the husband retains some rights. The passage provides two proofs for this: first, the wife only accepts liability when her self is surrendered (which happens in ba'in divorce), and second, it is an exchange of property for the self, requiring equality of ownership.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): An irrevocable divorce after which the husband cannot take back the wife without a new marriage.
تصرف معاوضہ (tasarruf mu'awada): A commutative transaction involving exchange of consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A khul' divorce is valid only when the husband offers a property-based divorce and the wife accepts, resulting in a ba'in divorce.

Invalid Consideration in Khul' and Divorce
Ruling on invalid consideration in khul' vs. explicit divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when khul' or explicit divorce is done with invalid consideration and explain why the divorce occurs but the consideration is void.

Core Idea
The divorce occurs regardless; the type depends on the word (kinayah vs sarih) and no compensation is due.

This lesson addresses a scenario where a husband divorces or performs khul' in exchange for something impermissible like wine, pig, or carrion. The Hanafi school distinguishes between khul' (a kinayah word) and explicit divorce (sarih), each with its own effect. We will explore why the consideration is not owed and why the divorce is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two cases: khul' with invalid consideration and explicit divorce (e.g. 'anti taliq') with invalid consideration. Note the different outcomes: ba'in for khul', raj'i for explicit divorce.
2
Analyze the reason for different outcomes
When the consideration is void, the operative word determines the type of divorce. 'Khul'' is kinayah, which in Hanafi law yields a ba'in divorce by default. Explicit words like 'anti taliq' yield a raj'i divorce. This is the central distinction.
3
Understand why no compensation is due
The wife did not specify a valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), so she is not considered a deceiver. Moreover, it is impossible to obligate the specified item (since it is impure) and impossible to obligate something else because she did not undertake it.
4
Compare with related cases
The text contrasts with: (a) khul' for specified vinegar that turns out to be wine – husband is deceived, wife must pay value of vinegar; (b) kitabah or freeing a slave for wine – slave's value is owed; (c) marriage for wine – the vulva is valuable in marriage, so compensation is required. In khul', the issue is removing ownership, which is honorable, so no compensation is needed.

مسلمان ہونے کی وجہ سے مسمی کو واجب نہیں کیا
The ruling for a Muslim regarding khul' in exchange for wine or pig. He (al-Quduri) said: If the consideration in khul' is invalid, such as a Muslim doing khul' for wine or pig or dead meat, then there is nothing for the husband and the separation is ba'in. And if the consideration is invalid in divorce, it becomes raj'i. So the occurrence of divorce in both cases is due to the suspension upon acceptance, and their difference in ruling is because when the consideration is invalid, in the first case the operative word is 'khul'', which is kinayah, and in the second it is explicit, which allows raj'a. And nothing is obligatory for the husband from her because she did not specify a valuable property such that she would be considered deceptive, and because there is no way to obligate the specified amount due to Islam, nor to obligate something else due to lack of undertaking. Contrary to the case where he did khul' for specified vinegar and it turned out to be wine, because she specified property so he is deceived. And contrary to the case where he made a kitabah or freed a slave for wine, where the value of the slave is owed because the master's ownership in that is valuable and he did not agree to its removal for free. As for the ownership of the vulva at the time of exit (khul'), it is not valuable, as we will mention. And contrary to marriage (for wine), because the vulva is valuable at the time of intercourse, and the wisdom is that the vulva is honorable, so its ownership is not legislated except for compensation to show its honor. As for the removal of ownership, it is itself honorable, so there is no need to obligate property. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And if the consideration in khul' is invalid, such as a Muslim doing khul' for wine or pig or dead meat, then there is nothing for the husband and the separation is ba'in. And if the consideration in divorce is invalid, the divorce is raj'i. So the occurrence of divorce in both cases is due to the suspension upon acceptance, and their difference in ruling is because when the consideration is invalid, in the first case the operative word is 'khul'', which is kinayah, and in the second it is explicit, which allows raj'a. And nothing is obligatory for the husband from her because she did not specify a valuable property such that she would be considered deceptive, and because there is no way to obligate the specified amount due to Islam, nor to obligate something else due to lack of undertaking. Contrary to the case where he did khul' for specified vinegar and it turned out to be wine, because she specified property so he is deceived. And contrary to the case where he made a kitabah or freed a slave for wine, where the value of the slave is owed because the master's ownership in that is valuable and he did not agree to its removal for free. As for the ownership of the vulva at the time of exit (khul'), it is not valuable, as we will mention. And contrary to marriage (for wine), because the vulva is valuable at the time of intercourse, and the wisdom is that the vulva is honorable, so its ownership is not legislated except for compensation to show its honor. As for the removal of ownership, it is itself honorable, so there is no need to obligate property. Explanation: The scenario is that if the consideration in khul' is invalid, for example a Muslim man does khul' with his wife for wine or pig or carrion, then nothing is obligatory for the husband from the wife as consideration, and a ba'in divorce will occur. And if he gave divorce to a consummated wife in exchange for consideration, and this is not the third divorce, and in this case the consideration is invalid, then a raj'i divorce will occur and nothing is obligatory for the husband from the wife. In both cases, the occurrence of divorce is because the wife's divorce was suspended upon her acceptance and she accepted. And the difference in ruling between the two cases is: in the first, a ba'in divorce occurs; in the second, a raj'i divorce. The reason for the difference is that when the consideration becomes invalid, in the first case the operative word is 'khul'', and 'khul'' is from the words of kinayah, and from kinayah words a ba'in divorce occurs, except for three words mentioned previously, and 'khul'' is not among them. So from the word 'khul'', a ba'in divorce occurs. In the second case, after the consideration becomes invalid, the explicit word 'anti taliq' (you are divorced) is operative, and from an explicit divorce, a raj'i divorce occurs. Therefore, in the second case a raj'i divorce occurs. And nothing is obligatory upon the wife because she did not specify any valuable property such that she would be considered deceptive. Since she is not deceptive, nothing is obligatory upon her. The second proof is that there are two ways to make consideration obligatory: either the specified amount is made obligatory, or something else is made obligatory. But both are impossible. First, because a Muslim cannot deliver wine, pig, etc., nor is it permissible to take possession of them legally, so due to being Muslim, the specified amount cannot be made obligatory.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya expands on the ruling when the consideration in khul' or divorce is invalid (like wine, pig, carrion). The main ruling: if khul' is done with invalid consideration, the divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) and no compensation is due. If an explicit divorce (like 'anti taliq') is given with invalid consideration, the divorce is raj'i (revocable) and no compensation is due. The difference stems from the words used: 'khul'' is a kinayah (indirect) word that implies ba'in divorce by default, while 'anti taliq' is a sarih (explicit) word that implies raj'i divorce. No compensation is due because the wife did not specify a valuable asset, so she is not considered a deceiver; also, it is impossible to obligate the specified item (since it is impure and cannot be owned by a Muslim) and impossible to obligate something else (since she didn't undertake it). The passage also compares with other cases: if she specified vinegar but it turned out to be wine, the husband is deceived and she must pay the value of vinegar. In kitabah or freeing a slave for wine, the slave's value is owed because the owner's property right is valuable. In marriage, the vulva is valuable at the time of intercourse, so compensation is required. But in khul', the issue is removing ownership (not acquiring it), which is itself honorable, so no compensation is needed.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kinayah): A word that implies divorce indirectly, requiring intention or context.
صریح (sarih): An explicit word for divorce (like 'anti taliq') that does not require intention.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is valuable and recognized in Islamic law as permissible to own and trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband performs khul' with his wife in exchange for a bottle of wine, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) No divorce occurs and the marriage continues.
B) A ba'in divorce occurs and the husband must pay the value of wine.
C) A ba'in divorce occurs and no compensation is owed.
D) A raj'i divorce occurs and no compensation is owed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, divorce or khul' with invalid consideration is valid; the divorce type depends on the word used, and no compensation is due.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Invalid 'Iwad in Khul' vs. Talaq
States the ruling when 'iwad is invalid in khul' and distinguishes from talaq with talil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the consideration ('iwad) in khul' or talaq is invalid, distinguish between ba'in and raj'i outcomes, and explain the legal reasoning (talil) based on the nature of the words used.

Core Idea
Invalid 'iwad does not void the divorce; it only affects whether it is ba'in (for khul') or raj'i (for talaq).

In this passage, al-Marghinani presents a nuanced ruling: if a Muslim husband makes khul' with an invalid consideration like wine, the divorce is valid and ba'in with no compensation due. But if he utters a sarih talaq with the same invalid consideration, the divorce is raj'i. The distinction hinges on whether the operative word is kinayah (khul') or sarih (talaq). Understanding this talil is essential for applying the ruling correctly in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize the two cases presented: khul' with invalid 'iwad vs. talaq (non-third) with invalid 'iwad. Both involve a Muslim husband and a consummated wife.
The 'iwad is invalid because it is not recognized as property for Muslims (e.g., wine, pig, carrion).
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For khul': the divorce is ba'in and no compensation is owed. For talaq: the divorce is raj'i and no compensation is owed.
The divorce is valid in both cases despite the invalid 'iwad.
3
Trace the talil for the different type of divorce
The difference in outcome (ba'in vs. raj'i) is due to the linguistic nature of the words used. Khul' is kinayah, which in Hanafi law gives a ba'in divorce (unless one of three exceptions). Talaq is sarih, which from a consummated marriage gives a raj'i divorce.
Kinayah words require intention; sarih words do not. This is a key principle.
4
Explain why no compensation is due
No compensation is owed because the wife did not specify a valuable property (mal muttaqawwim) that she could deliver. Even if she specified the invalid item, it cannot be delivered legally. An unspecified amount cannot be imposed without her acceptance.
Contrast with cases where compensation is due (e.g., specified vinegar that turns out spoiled) to reinforce the principle.
5
Note the contrast with other scenarios
The passage contrasts the ruling with three cases where liability does arise: (1) khul' on a specific jar of vinegar that turns out to be wine, (2) freeing a slave for wine where the slave's price is due, and (3) marriage on wine where mahr al-mithl is due. These highlight when consideration can be replaced with its value.
These distinctions will be covered in detail in subsequent lessons.

Because the vulva (bud') during the state of consummation is valuable (muttaqawwim), and the legal wisdom is that it is noble, so its ownership is not legislated except with a consideration to manifest its nobility. As for removing ownership, that is noble in itself, so there is no need to impose wealth. Translation: Al-Quduri said: If the consideration in khul' is invalid, such as a Muslim making khul' with wine, pig, or carrion, then the husband gets no consideration and it becomes a ba'in separation. If the consideration in talaq becomes invalid, then it becomes raj'i talaq. So in both cases divorce occurs because it was suspended on the woman's acceptance, and she accepted. The difference in ruling is that when the consideration became invalid, in the first case the operative word is khul' and it is kinayah, and in the second case the operative word is sarih, and after sarih talaq, raj'ah occurs. And the husband is not owed anything because the woman did not specify valuable property such that she would be considered deceiving him, and because there is no way to impose the named consideration due to being Muslim, and no way to impose the unnamed consideration due to lack of commitment. Unlike the case when khul' is made on a specific jar of vinegar and then it turns out to be spoiled, because the woman mentioned property, so the husband was deceived, and unlike the case when a master makes his slave a mukatab or frees him for wine, then the slave's price becomes due, because the master's property in the slave is valuable and he did not consent to destroy it for free. And the property in the bud' during the state of removal (khul') is non-valuable, as we will mention. And unlike marriage on wine, because during consummation the bud' is valuable. The secret is that the woman's bud' is a noble thing, so its ownership is not legislated except with a consideration to manifest its nobility, and as for removing ownership, that is noble in itself, so there is no need to impose wealth. Explanation of the issue: If in khul' the consideration is invalid, e.g., a Muslim man makes khul' with his wife for wine, pig, or carrion, then the husband is owed nothing as a substitute for khul', and the woman gets a ba'in divorce. And if a woman who has been consummated is given talaq for a consideration and it is not a third talaq, and the consideration is invalid, then the woman gets a raj'i talaq and the husband is owed nothing. In both cases divorce occurs because the woman's divorce was suspended on her acceptance and she accepted. The difference in ruling is that in the first case ba'in divorce occurs and in the second raj'i divorce. The reason for the difference: when the consideration becomes invalid, in the first case the operative word is khul' and it is from the words of kinayah, and from kinayah words ba'in divorce occurs except for three words mentioned previously, and the word khul' is not among them, so ba'in divorce occurs. In the second case, after invalidity of consideration, the word 'you are divorced' is sarih, and from sarih talaq, raj'i talaq occurs. The reason the woman is not owed anything is that she did not mention any valuable property such that she would be considered deceiving him, so when she is not deceiving, nothing is owed. Another proof: there are two ways to impose consideration: either the specified amount is imposed, or the unspecified amount is imposed. Both are impossible: first because a Muslim cannot hand over wine or pig and is not permitted to possess them legally, so due to being Muslim the specified cannot be imposed; second, the unspecified cannot be imposed because the woman did not undertake it.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof from al-Hidayah on the effect of invalid consideration in khul' versus talaq. The author first states the general rule: if a Muslim husband makes khul' with an invalid consideration (wine, pig, carrion), the husband receives nothing and the divorce is ba'in. In contrast, if he gives a talaq (not khul') with such an invalid consideration, the divorce is raj'i. The reason for the difference is purely linguistic: khul' is a kinayah word, while talaq is sarih. In Hanafi law, kinayah divorce is ba'in unless one of three exceptions, which do not include khul'. Sarih talaq from a consummated marriage is raj'i if not the third. The husband is not owed any compensation because the woman did not specify a valuable property (mal muttaqawwim) that would make her a deceiver. The text then shows that even if she had specified, she could not deliver it (wine is not property for Muslims) nor can she be forced to pay an unspecified amount because she did not undertake it. The passage also contrasts with three scenarios where liability does arise: a specific jar of vinegar that turns out to be wine (woman owes mahr al-mithl or vinegar value), a slave freed or made mukatab for wine (slave owes his price), and marriage on wine (mahr al-mithl is due). These exceptions will be detailed in subsequent lessons.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.
عوض باطل ('iwad batil): An invalid consideration that is not recognized as property in Islamic law, such as wine or pig for a Muslim.
صریح طلاق (sarih talaq): Explicit words of divorce like 'anti taliq' that effect a raj'i divorce if conditions are met.
کنایہ (kinayah): Allusive words that imply divorce but require intention; from such words ba'in divorce occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Muslim husband divorces his wife via khul' in exchange for wine, what is the ruling?
A) The khul' is invalid and no divorce occurs.
B) The khul' is valid and the husband is owed the value of wine.
C) The khul' is valid, the wife gets a ba'in divorce, and the husband gets nothing.
D) The khul' is valid and the wife gets a raj'i divorce.

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway is that invalid consideration does not void the divorce; it only impacts whether the divorce is ba'in or raj'i based on the wording used, and no compensation is due.

Three Exceptions: Liability Despite Invalid 'Iwad
Contrasts main rule with three exceptions where liability arises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three situations where an invalid 'iwad in khul', kitabah, or marriage does not void the contract and explain the Hanafi legal reasoning for each.

Core Idea
Invalid 'iwad does not always void the transaction—misrepresentation, property in slave, and the nature of bud' create liability in distinct ways.

The general rule in Hanafi law is that if the 'iwad (countervalue) in a contract is invalid (like wine), the contract is void and nothing is owed. However, there are three exceptions where the contract remains valid and compensation is due. This passage from al-Hidayah explains these exceptions: misrepresentation in khul', kitabah or emancipation for wine, and marriage for wine. Understanding these exceptions refines your grasp of when property (mal) and personal rights (bud') create obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exceptions
The passage contrasts three cases with the general rule that invalid 'iwad yields no liability. List the exceptions: (1) Khul' with misrepresented vinegar/wine, (2) Kitabah or emancipation for wine, (3) Marriage for wine. Note that in all three, the contract is valid and some compensation is due.
The general rule is not explicitly stated but is the baseline for comparison.
2
Analyze the first exception: misrepresented vinegar
The woman offers a specific jar thinking it is vinegar, but it is wine. She mentioned 'property' (mal) but the substance was not property. Therefore she deceived the husband, and deception in contract creates liability (daman). She is guarantor of the specified item, but since wine cannot be handed over, she must pay its value. Abu Hanifa says mahr al-mithl; the Sahibayn say the value of vinegar.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The text refers to the mahr chapter for details.
3
Analyze the second exception: slave's freedom for wine
If a master makes his slave a mukatab (contract for freedom) or frees him in exchange for wine, the slave owes his own price. The proof: the master's ownership in the slave is valuable (muttaqawwim). The master did not intend to lose his property for free, and the slave cannot deliver wine (non-property for a Muslim). So the substitute, i.e., the slave's price, is due.
No ikhtilaf among the Hanafi imams on this ruling.
4
Analyze the third exception: marriage vs. khul' for wine
If marriage is contracted for wine, it is valid and mahr al-mithl is due. If khul' is done for wine, it is valid but the woman owes nothing. The distinction: the bud' (vulva) is valuable at the time of entry (marriage) but non-valuable at the time of exit (khul'). The rationale: owning the bud' is noble, so compensation is required to show its dignity; whereas removing ownership (khul') is itself noble and does not require compensation.
This clarifies the difference in treatment between marriage and khul' regarding bud'.
5
Synthesize the common principle
In all three exceptions, something of value is being transferred or involved: in the first, the misrepresented property (deception triggers daman); in the second, the slave's valuable person; in the third, the bud' at entry. When the 'iwad is invalid, the law ensures that the counterparty is not left uncompensated by substituting a legitimate value.
This shows that Hanafi law protects property and personal rights even when the specific 'iwad fails.

Source Text
